```json
{"file_path": "/var/data/inverted-pendulum/pendulum/balance.py", "content": "\"\"\"Balance (stabilize upright) controllers: discrete LQR with either\nfinite-difference velocity estimation or a steady-state Kalman filter.\n\nState for LQR: z = [theta (n), thetad (n), x, v], input a = xdd.\nThe controller outputs a pivot-velocity command v_cmd = v + a*dt.\n\"\"\"\n\nfrom __future__ import annotations\n\nimport numpy as np\nfrom scipy.linalg import solve_discrete_are\nfrom scipy.signal import cont2discrete\n\nfrom .dynamics import Chain\n\n\ndef dlqr(Ad, Bd, Q, R):\n    P = solve_discrete_are(Ad, Bd, Q, R)\n    K = np.linalg.solve(R + Bd.T @ P @ Bd, Bd.T @ P @ Ad)\n    return K, P\n\n\ndef upright_lqr(chain: Chain, dt: float, q_theta=10.0, q_thetad=1.0, q_x=0.1, q_v=0.5, r=0.1):\n    \"\"\"Discrete LQR gain about the upright equilibrium.\"\"\"\n    Ac, Bc = chain.linearize_upright()\n    Ad, Bd, *_ = cont2discrete((Ac, Bc, np.eye(Ac.shape[0]), 0), dt)\n    n = chain.n\n    Q = np.diag([q_theta] * n + [q_thetad] * n + [q_x, q_v])\n    R = np.array([[r]])\n    K, P = dlqr(Ad, Bd, Q, R)\n    return K, P, Ad, Bd\n\n\nclass FDBalancer:\n    \"\"\"LQR with finite-difference velocity estimate from quantized angles.\"\"\"\n\n    def __init__(self, chain: Chain, dt: float, **lqr_kw):\n        self.K, self.P, *_ = upright_lqr(chain, dt, **lqr_kw)\n        self.n = chain.n\n        self.dt = dt\n        self.prev = None\n\n    def reset(self):\n        self.prev = None\n\n    def __call__(
```

```json
{"file_path": "/var/data/inverted-pendulum/scripts/smoke_balance.py", "content": "\"\"\"Smoke test: LQR balance for N=1..5 with perfect observation, plus\nunstable-pole analysis of the upright equilibrium.\"\"\"\n\nimport sys, pathlib\n\nsys.path.insert(0, str(pathlib.Path(__file__).resolve().parents[1]))\nimport numpy as np\n\nfrom pendulum.dynamics import Chain\nfrom pendulum.sim import simulate, upright_fail_check\nfrom pendulum.balance import FDBalancer, KalmanBalancer\n\nDT = 0.01\nG = 9.81\n\nfor n in range(1, 6):\n    chain = Chain(n, G)\n    Ac, _ = chain.linearize_upright()\n    eig = np.linalg.eigvals(Ac)\n    lam_max = np.max(eig.real)\n    y0 = np.zeros(2 * n)\n    y0[:n] = 0.02 * (np.arange(n) % 2 * 2 - 1)  # alternating 0.02 rad tilt\n    for name, ctrl in [\n        (\"FD \", FDBalancer(chain, DT)),\n        (\"KF \", KalmanBalancer(chain, DT, dtheta=0.0, dv=0.0)),\n    ]:\n        res = simulate(\n            chain, ctrl, y0, DT, n_steps=int(30 / DT),\n            fail_check=upright_fail_check(chain),\n        )\n        final_tilt = np.max(np.abs(res[\"y\"][:n]))\n        print(\n            f\"N={n} {name} lam_max={lam_max:5.2f}/s  success={res['success']}\"\n            f\"  t={res['t']:5.1f}s  final max|theta|={final_tilt:.2e}  v={res['v']:+.2e}\"\n        )\n"}
```

All workstreams are now running in parallel. Status summary:

**Done & committed:**
- **Dynamics core** — closed-form EOM for the N-link chain with velocity-driven pivot, derived by hand: $M(\theta)\ddot\theta = -\ddot{x}\,b\cos\theta - A\sin(\theta_i-\theta_l)\,\dot\theta_l^2 + g\,b\sin\theta$. Verified against an independent sympy Lagrangian derivation (N=1–5, error <1e-8) and energy conservation (drift <5e-9 over 10 s).
- **Simulator** — RK4, ZOH acceleration from velocity commands, quantization hooks for angle measurements (`dtheta`) and velocity commands (`dv`).
- **Balance controllers** — discrete LQR (finite-difference and Kalman-filter variants). All N=1–5 balance with perfect observation. Key finding already: the upright basin shrinks dramatically with N (0.02 rad alternating tilt is unrecoverable for N≥4; unstable pole grows 3.8→15.9 /s from N=1→5).
- **Shared protocol** — precision = largest quantization step that still succeeds, found by log-bisection over 8 (balance) / 4 (swing-up) seeded trials.

**Running in background (3 agents + 1 job):**
1. **Balance precision sweeps** — max `dtheta`, max `dv`, joint trade-off curves, N=1–5, both controllers, plus dt-scaling (0.002–0.02) and g-scaling.
2. **N=1 swing-up** — energy pumping + LQR catch, then its precision sweep.
3. **N=2–5 swing-up** — CasADi direct collocation for nominal trajectories, TVLQR tracking, LQR catch, then precision sweeps. This is the hard one (N=4–5 especially).
4. **Basin measurement** — basin radii + linear noise-amplification factor κ per N, so we can compare empirical thresholds against the theory prediction `dtheta_max ≈ basin/κ`.

I'll report as each completes.

```
The search results reveal significant work on data rate theorems and quantized feedback stabilization. Here's what the research shows:

## Key Results

Nair and Evans (2004) and Tatikonda and Mitter (2004) established that there exists a tight bound on the data rate for stabilization of linear systems, which is expressed simply by the product of the unstable poles of the plant.

## Background and Context

Tatikonda and Mitter studied a model in which the rate is deterministic and system disturbances are bounded, and this work was later extended by Nair and Evans to the more realistic case in which the rate is deterministic, but disturbances can have an unbounded support.

Nair and Evans obtained their result by considering the stabilization of the noiseless discrete-time system by feedback through a quantized channel in which the quantizer is seen as an information encoder, and showed that the condition is necessary and sufficient for the existence of a coding and control law that gives exponential convergence of the state.

## Related Work

Subsequent works showed that this index gives the minimum channel quality for stabilization in various situations, with later researchers deriving the minimum SNR required for mean-square stabilization through additive white Gaussian noise (AWGN) channels.

The foundational papers by these researchers have become central to understanding fundamental limitations in networked control systems.
```

```
## Key Research Findings

Human stick balancing is investigated in terms of reaction time delay and sensory dead zones for position and velocity perception using a special combination of delayed state feedback and mismatched predictor feedback as a control model.

### Sensory Dead Zones and Quantization Effects

Quantization and control torque saturation have a stabilizing effect: the region of stabilizing control gains is greater than that of the linear model. Control torque saturation allows the application of larger control gains without overcontrol while sensory quantization plays a role of a kind of filter when sensory noise is present.

The effect of the sensory dead zone is to produce a hybrid type of control in which the PD feedback is switched ON or OFF depending on whether or not the controlled variable is larger or smaller than the detection threshold.

### Critical Delay and Human Balancing

For seated expert stick balancers, the time delay is 0.23 s, the shortest stick that can be balanced for 240 s is 0.32 m and there is a dead zone for the estimation of the vertical displacement angle in the saggital plane.

Due to the sensory dead zones, initial conditions of the actual state cannot always be provided for an internal-model-based prediction, which indicates that perfect prediction is not possible and the delay in the switching condition cannot be compensated.
```

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:26:15; model 48.6s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Yes — quite a lot, across three mostly separate literatures. Our specific quantitative question (precision thresholds vs. N for *velocity-only* pivot control) doesn't seem to have a direct published answer, but each piece is well studied:

## 1. Quantized control & data-rate theorems — the theory side of "required precision"

This is the closest formal framing of your question. The celebrated **data-rate theorem** ([Tatikonda & Mitter 2004; Nair & Evans 2004](https://link.springer.com/rwe/10.1007/978-3-030-44184-5_149)) says a linear system can be stabilized over a feedback channel iff the data rate exceeds the sum of the unstable poles:

$$R > \sum_i \frac{\lambda_i^+}{\ln 2} \;\text{bits/s} \quad\Longleftrightarrow\quad \text{bits per sample} > \frac{\Delta t}{\ln 2}\sum_i \lambda_i^+$$

This directly predicts how required precision depends on the simulation parameters: quantizing N angles with step δθ over range R gives ~N·log₂(R/δθ) bits/sample, and the unstable-pole sum Σλ⁺ grows with N and as √g (it's 3.8 → 15.9 s⁻¹ for our N=1→5 at g=9.81 — I computed these earlier). It gives a *lower bound* (a floor no controller can beat); our sweeps measure the practical threshold above it. Earlier roots: Delchamps (1990) on quantized state feedback, Brockett & Liberzon (2000), Elia & Mitter (2001) on logarithmic quantization, and [tightest-bound work by Baillieul and others](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0167691100000372).

## 2. Multi-link pendulum swing-up — the robotics side

- **Double pendulum on cart**: Furuta's classic work; Åström & Furuta's energy-based swing-up (the method our N=1 agent uses); Graichen et al. (2007) feedforward/feedback swing-up.
- **Triple pendulum on cart**: the landmark is [Glück, Eder & Kugi 2013, *Automatica* — swing-up of a triple pendulum with experimental validation](https://www.acin.tuwien.ac.at/file/publications/cds/pre_post_print/glueck2013.pdf). Their architecture — nonlinear feedforward trajectory + optimal time-varying feedback — is exactly the pipeline our N=2–5 agent is building (direct collocation + TVLQR + catch).
- **Quadruple**: much rarer — [an optimal-control study](https://jte.edu.vn/index.php/jte/article/view/1025) and recently [RL achieving real-time control of a quadruple inverted pendulum (2025)](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s12555-025-0235-y). Quintuple on a cart is essentially unexplored territory experimentally — simulation studies only.

## 3. Human stick balancing — quantization/dead-zone effects in the wild

Milton & Insperger have a long program treating human balancing as control with **sensory dead zones, delays, and quantization**: [predictor feedback with dead zones](https://pubs.aip.org/aip/cha/article-abstract/32/5/053108/2835677/Predictor-feedback-models-for-stick-balancing-with), [sensorimotor uncertainty in virtual stick balancing](https://royalsocietypublishing.org/doi/10.1098/rsos.191006), [expert balancing at stability's edge](https://royalsocietypublishing.org/rsif/article/13/119/20160212/35673/Control-at-stability-s-edge-minimizes-energetic). Two findings particularly relevant to us:
- **"Microchaos"** ([Csernák/Gyebrószki/Stépán school](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/327257226_Microchaos_in_human_postural_balance_Sensory_dead_zones_and_sampled_time-delayed_feedback)): sampled feedback + quantization produces small chaotic limit cycles around the equilibrium instead of convergence — exactly the behavior I expect our balance sims to show near threshold.
- Counterintuitively, [quantization can mildly *stabilize*](https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/The-effects-of-sensory-quantization-and-control-on-Gyebr%C3%B3szki-Csern%C3%A1k/fe5c4e340449ecf1032eee42aa7080b31ce2fbc9) by acting as a filter in the presence of noise.

Also adjacent but mechanistically different: Kapitza-style vibrational stabilization (Acheson & Mullin balanced a triple pendulum open-loop with vertical vibration), and geometric control of chain pendula (Lee/Leok/McClamroch).

**Where our experiment sits:** the data-rate theorem gives the information-theoretic floor and a predicted scaling (δθ_max should shrink roughly exponentially in Σλ⁺·Δt, and basin shrinkage adds to that); the robotics literature stops at N=3–4 and rarely quantifies sensor-precision requirements; the microchaos literature does quantify quantization effects but only for N=1. The N=1–5 precision-threshold table we're producing — with velocity-only actuation — is a genuinely under-charted corner. When the sweeps finish, comparing the measured dt-scaling against the data-rate prediction will be one of the more interesting parts of the report.

Sources: [Quantized Control and Data Rate Constraints (Springer encyclopedia)](https://link.springer.com/rwe/10.1007/978-3-030-44184-5_149) · [Nair data-rate theorem notes](https://people.eng.unimelb.edu.au/gnair/Allerton-control.pdf) · [Stabilization with data-rate-limited feedback](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S0167691100000372) · [Glück et al. 2013](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/abs/pii/S000510981200605X) ([PDF](https://www.acin.tuwien.ac.at/file/publications/cds/pre_post_print/glueck2013.pdf)) · [Quadruple optimal control](https://jte.edu.vn/index.php/jte/article/view/1025) · [RL quadruple pendulum 2025](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s12555-025-0235-y) · [Predictor feedback with dead zones](https://pubs.aip.org/aip/cha/article-abstract/32/5/053108/2835677/Predictor-feedback-models-for-stick-balancing-with) · [Virtual stick balancing](https://royalsocietypublishing.org/doi/10.1098/rsos.191006) · [Microchaos in postural balance](https://www.researchgate.net/publication/327257226_Microchaos_in_human_postural_balance_Sensory_dead_zones_and_sampled_time-delayed_feedback) · [Sensory quantization effects](https://www.semanticscholar.org/paper/The-effects-of-sensory-quantization-and-control-on-Gyebr%C3%B3szki-Csern%C3%A1k/fe5c4e340449ecf1032eee42aa7080b31ce2fbc9) · [Stability's edge balancing](https://royalsocietypublishing.org/rsif/article/13/119/20160212/35673/Control-at-stability-s-edge-minimizes-energetic)

| 3 | 10.3 | 0.066 rad | 63  | ~1·10⁻³ rad |
| 4 | 13.2 | 0.014 rad | 297 | ~5·10⁻⁵ rad |
| 5 | 15.9 | 0.0033 rad | 1341 | ~2.5·10⁻⁶ rad |

Two multiplicative mechanisms compound per added link: the basin of attraction shrinks ~5×/link **and** the closed-loop amplification of measurement noise grows ~5×/link — so required angle precision should tighten roughly **25–40× per link**. If that holds, balancing 5 links at dt=10 ms requires micro-radian-class angle sensing, which matches the data-rate-theorem intuition (Σλ⁺ grows superlinearly in N) and explains why nobody has built a quintuple pendulum.

### 👤 **User**  ·  17:31:16; waited 4m21s

do these numbers depend on timestep or gravity?

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:31:46; model 48.3s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Yes — but in a structured way. There's an exact scaling symmetry that separates the two:

## Gravity: an exact rescaling symmetry

With L=1 fixed, the equations of motion are $M\ddot\theta = -\ddot x\, b\cos\theta - A\sin(\Delta\theta)\dot\theta^2 + g\,b\sin\theta$. Rescaling time by $\tau = 1/\sqrt{g}$ maps the system at gravity $g$ onto the system at $g{=}1$: angles unchanged, velocities scale by $\sqrt g$, accelerations by $g$. So a simulation with parameters $(g,\ dt,\ \delta\theta,\ \delta v)$ is *exactly equivalent* to one with $(1,\ dt\sqrt g,\ \delta\theta,\ \delta v/\sqrt g)$. Consequences:

$$\boxed{\;\delta\theta_{max}(g, dt) = f\!\left(dt\sqrt{g/L}\right), \qquad \delta v_{max}(g, dt) = \sqrt{gL}\cdot h\!\left(dt\sqrt{g/L}\right)\;}$$

- **Unstable poles scale exactly as $\sqrt g$**: for N=1, $\lambda = \sqrt{3g/2} = 3.84$/s at g=9.81 — that's where the table's λ column comes from. Stronger gravity = proportionally faster instability.
- **Angle quantities (basin radius, δθ threshold) are dimensionless** — gravity affects them *only* through the combination $dt\sqrt g$. Doubling gravity is equivalent to slowing your sampling by √2.
- **Velocity quantities carry an explicit $\sqrt g$ prefactor** — at fixed $dt\sqrt g$, stronger gravity actually *loosens* the absolute velocity-precision requirement (the natural velocity scale $\sqrt{gL}$ grows), though the relative precision stays fixed.

(One caveat: this is exact for the *plant*; our LQR weights aren't written in rescaled units, so the controller isn't perfectly scale-covariant. The g-sweeps in agent A's protocol will show how close to exact the collapse is in practice.)

## Timestep: real dependence, two regimes

The numbers in the table were computed at dt=0.01, and dt enters genuinely:

1. **Hard ceiling**: sampled control fails outright when $\lambda_{max}\, dt$ gets too large — you must sample several times per e-folding of the instability. For N=5 ($\lambda \approx 15.9$/s) that's dt ≲ a few tens of ms; at dt=0.01 we're at $\lambda dt \approx 0.16$, comfortable but not lavish.
2. **Precision–rate trade-off below the ceiling**: the data-rate theorem says what matters is bits per *second*, $\frac{N}{dt}\log_2(\text{range}/\delta\theta) \gtrsim \sum_i\lambda_i^+/\ln 2$ — so in principle, faster sampling tolerates coarser quantization. In practice it's controller-dependent and non-monotonic: with finite-difference velocity estimation, halving dt *doubles* the velocity-estimate noise from a given δθ (error ~ δθ/dt), while a Kalman filter averages across samples and recovers the theoretical benefit. This is exactly why I had agent A sweep dt ∈ {0.002, 0.005, 0.01, 0.02} with both estimator types.

Of the specific table columns: **λ_max** is dt-independent (continuous-time property, ∝√g); **basin radius** is nearly dt-independent while $\lambda dt \ll 1$ (it's a property of the nonlinear closed loop, and our dt is well inside that regime); **κ** depends on dt through the discrete design but converges to the continuous LQG limit as dt→0.

So the honest summary: the table is the $(g{=}9.81,\ dt{=}0.01)$ slice; theory says everything should collapse onto curves in the single dimensionless variable $dt\sqrt{g/L}$ (plus a √g prefactor on velocity thresholds), and the running sweeps will test that collapse empirically. When they land, I'll plot thresholds vs. $dt\sqrt g$ to check.

The trend fit is extremely regular (R² in log-space is near-perfect: basin shrinks 3.9×/link, κ grows 5.2×/link, predicted δθ shrinks **20.3×/link**). Direct computation of N=6,7 is running to verify the extrapolation; here's the answer, which I'll confirm against the computed values when it lands in a couple of minutes.

## Extended table (N=6,7 extrapolated from the fitted trends)

| N | λ_max (1/s) | Σλ⁺ (1/s) | basin (rad) | κ | **required δθ ≈ basin/κ (rad)** |
|---|------|------|---------|---------|---------|
| 1 | 3.84 | 3.8 | 0.70 | 1.7 | ~4·10⁻¹ |
| 2 | 7.19 | 9.9 | 0.24 | 12 | ~2·10⁻² |
| 3 | 10.25 | 17.9 | 0.066 | 63 | ~1·10⁻³ |
| 4 | 13.15 | 27.6 | 0.014 | 297 | ~5·10⁻⁵ |
| 5 | 15.85 | 38.8 | 0.0033 | 1341 | ~2.5·10⁻⁶ |
| **6** | **18.34** (exact) | **51.3** (exact) | ~9·10⁻⁴ | ~8·10³ | **~1.2·10⁻⁷** |
| **7** | **20.63** (exact) | **65.0** (exact) | ~2.4·10⁻⁴ | ~4·10⁴ | **~6·10⁻⁹** |

(λ_max and Σλ⁺ for N=6,7 are exact — computed from the linearization, not extrapolated. λ_max ≈ (3.0N + 1.06)·√(g/9.81) — strikingly linear in N.)

So at g=9.81, dt=10 ms: **N=6 needs ~0.1 µrad-class angle sensing, N=7 needs ~6 nrad** — the latter is below the diffraction-limited pointing stability of most real instruments; N≈6 is plausibly the hard physical ceiling for this sensing/actuation architecture at this rate.

## Dependence on gravity and timestep

**Exact symmetry** (plant-level, with rod length L restored): time-rescaling by $\sqrt{g/L}$ maps gravities onto each other. Every threshold must therefore take the form

$$\boxed{\ \delta\theta_{\max}(N, g, dt) = \Phi_N\!\big(u\big), \qquad \delta v_{\max}(N, g, dt) = \sqrt{gL}\;\Psi_N\!\big(u\big), \qquad u \equiv dt\sqrt{g/L}\ }$$

Gravity **does not appear independently** in the angle threshold — only through $u$. The velocity threshold gets one explicit $\sqrt{gL}$ prefactor (the natural velocity unit).

**The shape of Φ and Ψ** — three regimes in $u$, with the mechanism for each:

1. **Sampling ceiling** ($u \gtrsim 1/\hat\lambda_N$, where $\hat\lambda_N = \lambda_{max}/\sqrt{g/L} \approx 3.0N{+}1$ dimensionless): control fails at *any* precision. The pendulum e-folds away between samples. Hard bound: $dt < c/(N\sqrt{g/L})$, empirically with $c \sim 0.5\!-\!1$.

2. **Working regime** ($u \ll 1/\hat\lambda_N$, ours: $u = 0.031$): the basin/amplification mechanism dominates. The N-dependence factorizes out of the fitted data:
$$\delta\theta_{\max} \approx 8 \cdot 20^{-N}\, \phi(u)\ \text{rad}, \qquad \phi(0.031)=1$$
The dt-dependence $\phi(u)$ is *estimator-dependent*, which is exactly why agent A is sweeping it:
   - **Finite-difference velocity estimate**: velocity noise ~ δθ/dt, so amplification κ ∝ 1/dt and $\phi(u) \propto u$ — i.e. δθ_max ∝ dt·√g. *Faster sampling demands proportionally finer sensors.*
   - **Kalman estimate**: quantization acts as white measurement noise; its continuous-time intensity is δθ²·dt/12, so the induced angle wander ~ δθ·√(λ̂u) and $\phi(u) \propto 1/\sqrt{u}$ — *faster sampling relaxes the sensor requirement* (averaging wins), saturating at the basin radius itself as u→0.

3. **Actuator (velocity-command) quantization**: each step injects an angular-velocity kick ∝ δv; the kicks accumulate diffusively at rate δv²/dt, so survival demands roughly
$$\delta v_{\max} \sim \sqrt{gL}\cdot r_N\, \hat\lambda_N^{3/2}\sqrt{u} \quad\Rightarrow\quad \delta v_{\max} \propto g^{3/4}\sqrt{dt}$$
Counterintuitively, *slower* sampling tolerates coarser velocity quantization (fewer kicks per second), and *stronger gravity helps* in absolute terms (the natural velocity scale grows faster than the instability does).

**Information-theoretic floor** underneath it all (data-rate theorem, using the Σλ⁺ column): $\frac{N}{dt}\log_2\frac{\Theta}{\delta\theta} \ge \frac{\sum\lambda^+}{\ln 2}$ gives $\delta\theta \le \Theta\, e^{-u\,\hat S_N/N}$ with $\hat S_N = \Sigma\lambda^+/\sqrt{g/L}$. At our operating point this floor is far looser than the basin/κ bound — meaning the binding constraint is *dynamics* (noise amplification through the unstable plant), not *information*. No controller redesign can beat the floor, but a smarter controller can approach it; the gap (~4 orders of magnitude at N=5) is the price of using a simple LQG-type architecture.

Caveats on the extrapolation, honestly stated: the N=6,7 basin/κ rows assume the log-linear trend continues (the direct computation now running will confirm); the regime-2/3 exponents for $\phi,\psi$ are theory pending agent A's empirical dt-sweep confirmation; and all "required precision" numbers are for the best controller we've built, not a proven optimum over all controllers — the true requirement lies between our measurement and the data-rate floor.

### 👤 **User**  ·  17:41:30; waited 3m31s

what's Kappa here?

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:41:40; model 24.9s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

κ is the **closed-loop noise amplification factor**: how much a lie in the angle *measurement* gets converted into real motion of the *actual* angles, worst-case over frequencies. It's dimensionless (rad of true wobble per rad of measurement error).

**Mechanism.** The controller can't distinguish measurement error from real tilt. When the sensor reads θ+e instead of θ, the LQR reacts to the phantom component e with a real control action $a = -K_\theta e$ — it physically shoves the pendulum to "correct" an error that doesn't exist. That wrong shove propagates through the unstable plant, producing genuine angle deviation, which gets measured (with new quantization error), reacted to again, and so on around the loop.

**Definition** (as computed in `scripts/basin.py`): take the closed loop $z_{k+1} = (A_d - B_dK)z_k - B_dK_\theta e_k$ where $e_k$ is the perturbation on the measured angles. κ is the peak gain of the transfer function from $e$ to the true-angle part of $z$:

$$\kappa = \max_{\omega\in[0,\pi]} \ \bar\sigma\Big( \big[(e^{j\omega}I - A_d + B_dK)^{-1}B_dK_\theta\big]_{\theta\text{-rows}} \Big)$$

i.e. the discrete-time $H_\infty$ norm of the measurement-error→angle sensitivity, with $\bar\sigma$ the largest singular value (worst direction of error across the N sensor channels).

**Why it predicts the precision threshold.** Quantization with step δθ is bounded measurement error, |e| ≤ δθ/2. The closed loop turns that into sustained real oscillation of amplitude up to ~κ·δθ. The loop survives only while that oscillation stays inside the basin of attraction, giving the failure criterion

$$\kappa\,\delta\theta \gtrsim r_N \quad\Longrightarrow\quad \delta\theta_{max} \approx r_N/\kappa$$

— the last column of the table. (It's a linear-theory estimate; the empirical sweeps will calibrate the O(1) constant.)

**Why it explodes with N** (1.7 → 26,000 from N=1→7): stabilizing N unstable poles up to λ≈3N/s with a *single* scalar input forces very high feedback gains (the LQR gain matrix grows from ~10 at N=1 to ~10⁵ at N=7), and high gain on the measurement is precisely high gain on the measurement *error* — there's no way to react hard to real tilt while reacting gently to phantom tilt at the same frequencies. That's the fundamental sensitivity trade-off (Bode-integral-type constraint: aggregate sensitivity is lower-bounded by the unstable pole sum, which grows like N²√g).

So the per-link precision factor ~20 decomposes as: basin shrinks ~4×/link (nonlinear geometry gets tighter) **times** κ grows ~5×/link (the controller must listen ~5× harder to its sensors).

Good timing — that's the other half of the precision question. The empirical sweep (agent A) is still running, but I can compute the theory-side prediction right now, the same way as for angles. The key subtlety: a velocity-command quantization error $e \in [\pm\delta v/2]$ becomes an **acceleration** error $e/dt$ (because the sim realizes commands via $a = (v_{cmd}-v)/dt$), so the input-error gain carries a 1/dt factor. Let me compute the input-channel amplification κ_v per N:

**🔧 tool → Bash**  ·  3.0s
```json
{"command": "uv run python - <<'EOF' > runs/kappa_v.log 2>&1\nimport sys, json; sys.path.insert(0, '.')\nimport numpy as np\nfrom pendulum.dynamics import Chain\nfrom pendulum.balance import upright_lqr\n\nDT, G = 0.01, 9.81\nbasin = {**json.load(open('results/basin.json')), **json.load(open('results/basin_N6-7.json'))}\nprint(f\"{'N':>2} {'kappa_v (rad/(m/s))':>20} {'basin':>9} {'pred dv_max (m/s)':>18}\")\nfor n in range(1, 8):\n    ch = Chain(n, G)\n    K, P, Ad, Bd = upright_lqr(ch, DT)\n    Acl = Ad - Bd @ K\n    nz = Acl.shape[0]\n    # velocity-command error e -> acceleration error e/dt through input channel\n    kv = 0.0\n    for w in np.linspace(0, np.pi, 600):\n        zinv = np.linalg.inv(np.exp(1j*w)*np.eye(nz) - Acl)\n        Tn = (zinv @ Bd)[:n, :] / DT\n        kv = max(kv, float(np.abs(Tn).max()))\n    r = basin[str(n)]['basin_alt']\n    print(f\"{n:>2} {kv:>20.1f} {r:>9.1e} {r/kv:>18.1e}\")\nEOF\ncat runs/kappa_v.log", "description": "Compute velocity-command noise amplification and predicted dv thresholds"}
```

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:43:56; model 44.4s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Computed. And the result reveals a sharp asymmetry with the angle requirement:

## Velocity-command quantization limit (balance, g=9.81, dt=0.01)

| N | κ_v (rad per m/s) | basin (rad) | **required δv ≈ basin/κ_v** | ~bits of actuator DAC* |
|---|------|---------|----------|------|
| 1 | 4.7 | 0.70 | **~1.5·10⁻¹ m/s** | 5 |
| 2 | 4.2 | 0.24 | **~5.9·10⁻² m/s** | 7 |
| 3 | 4.0 | 0.066 | **~1.6·10⁻² m/s** | 9 |
| 4 | 4.1 | 0.014 | **~3.4·10⁻³ m/s** | 11 |
| 5 | 4.3 | 0.0033 | **~7.7·10⁻⁴ m/s** | 13 |
| 6 | 4.6 | 7.0·10⁻⁴ | **~1.5·10⁻⁴ m/s** | 15 |
| 7 | 5.1 | 1.4·10⁻⁴ | **~2.8·10⁻⁵ m/s** | 18 |

*bits to cover a ±2 m/s command range. Empirical anchor: at N=1, agent B measured 0.22 m/s for balance vs 0.15 predicted — theory is mildly conservative, right order. Swing-up tightens this ~4× at N=1 (0.054 m/s, catch-limited); expect a similar catch penalty at higher N.

## The interesting structural finding: κ_v ≈ 4–5, flat in N

Unlike the sensor amplification κ_θ (which explodes 1.7 → 26,000), the actuator-error amplification is essentially **constant across N**. So:

- **Angle precision requirement: shrinks ~20×/link** (basin ~4× × κ_θ ~5×)
- **Velocity precision requirement: shrinks only ~4×/link** (basin shrinkage alone)

The reason is fundamental, not accidental: a velocity-command error enters the plant through *the same channel as the control itself* — and rejecting input-channel disturbances is precisely what the feedback loop is designed to do, so its gain there stays O(1) no matter how aggressive the controller gets. A sensor error instead corrupts the *information* the loop acts on, and the high-gain feedback needed for many links amplifies it unavoidably. **Sensing, not actuation, is the binding constraint for large N** — at N=5 you need ~19 bits of angle sensing but only ~13 bits of velocity actuation; the gap widens by ~2.3 bits per link.

## Dependence on g and dt

From the exact rescaling symmetry: $\delta v_{max} = \sqrt{gL}\;\Psi_N(dt\sqrt{g/L})$.

- **Gravity**: explicit √g prefactor — *stronger gravity loosens the absolute velocity requirement* (the natural velocity scale √(gL) grows, while the angle-space geometry is g-invariant). Agent B's N=1 data: 0.041 / 0.054 / 0.058 m/s at g = 4.9 / 9.81 / 19.6 — consistent with √g at the low end, flattening at high g where the catch dynamics bite.
- **Timestep**: a δv quantum is an acceleration error δv/dt lasting one step, i.e. a velocity kick of fixed size δv but delivered *more often* at small dt — error injection is diffusive at rate ~δv²/dt. So **smaller dt demands finer velocity quantization**, roughly δv_max ∝ √dt to dt; agent B measured 0.016 / 0.035 / 0.054 m/s at dt = 2 / 5 / 10 ms (≈ sublinear, between √dt and linear). Opposite sign of the Kalman-filtered angle requirement, where faster sampling *helps*.

One caveat: κ_v is a worst-case (H∞) bound treating quantization as adversarial at the worst frequency; real quantization error is partially self-dithered by the loop, which is why the empirical thresholds run slightly looser than basin/κ_v. The full empirical δv table for N=1–5 (both estimators, with the non-monotonicity hardening) lands when agent A finishes.

## Sensor (angle) precision requirement — balance, g=9.81, dt=0.01, L=1

| N | λ_max (1/s) | basin radius (rad) | κ_θ (rad/rad) | **required δθ ≈ basin/κ_θ (rad)** | ~bits/channel* |
|---|------|---------|---------|----------|------|
| 1 | 3.84 | 0.70 | 1.7 | **~4·10⁻¹** | 2 |
| 2 | 7.19 | 0.24 | 12 | **~2·10⁻²** | 6 |
| 3 | 10.25 | 0.066 | 63 | **~1·10⁻³** | 10 |
| 4 | 13.15 | 0.014 | 297 | **~5·10⁻⁵** | 15 |
| 5 | 15.85 | 0.0033 | 1,341 | **~2.5·10⁻⁶** | 19 |
| 6 | 18.34 | 7.0·10⁻⁴ | 5,948 | **~1.2·10⁻⁷** | 23 |
| 7 | 20.63 | 1.4·10⁻⁴ | 26,136 | **~5.5·10⁻⁹** | 28 |

*bits to resolve δθ over a ±π measurement range, per link.

Notes:
- **Fit**: δθ_max ≈ 8·20⁻ᴺ rad — about **20× finer per added link** (basin shrinks ~4×/link, amplification κ_θ grows ~5×/link).
- N=1–5 rows are direct measurements of basin and κ; N=6–7 were computed directly too (they happened to match the log-linear extrapolation almost exactly). λ_max ≈ (3.0N + 1.06)·√(g/9.81) — exact from the linearization.
- **Empirical anchors so far** (full N=1–5 sweep still running): N=1 balance measured ≈ 0.52 rad vs ~0.4 predicted ✓; N=1 *swing-up* tightens it to ≈ 0.024 rad (catch-limited, ~22× stricter than balance).
- **Parameter dependence**: δθ_max = Φ_N(dt·√(g/L)) — gravity enters *only* through the product dt·√g. With Kalman filtering, faster sampling relaxes the requirement (∝ ~1/√dt until it saturates at the basin radius); with naive finite-difference velocity estimation it tightens instead (∝ dt).
- Physical reading: ~10⁻³ rad (N=3) is ordinary encoder territory, ~2.5·10⁻⁶ rad (N=5) is interferometric, ~5·10⁻⁹ rad (N=7) is below realistic sensing — N≈6 is plausibly the practical ceiling at this rate, and that's *before* the swing-up penalty (~one extra order of magnitude at N=1; higher-N penalty TBD when agent C's trajectory-optimization results land).

### 👤 **User**  ·  17:47:57; waited 3m01s

if I read this correctly, it's not possible to balance a 6-link inverted pendulum with fp32 theta?

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:48:36; model 47.8s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Almost — but there's a crucial subtlety: my table is for **uniform** quantization (encoder-style, fixed absolute step), while fp32 has *relative* precision — its absolute resolution shrinks as θ→0, which is exactly where a balancing pendulum lives. So the answer depends on the representation. This is cheap to test empirically — let me just run it:

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:51:30; model 7.6s

To summarize the answer to your question: **fp32 θ is sufficient — N=6 and even N=7 balance fine with fp32 measurements**, in both representations tested. The earlier table was the worst-case (H∞) theoretical bound, and the direct experiment showed it's ~2 orders of magnitude conservative: the *measured* uniform-quantization limits are ~5·10⁻⁵ rad (N=6) and ~2·10⁻⁵ rad (N=7), with the per-link tightening factor closer to 5× than 20×. fp32-from-upright benefits additionally from its contracting absolute resolution near θ=0 and would only fail around N≈10+; fp32-from-hanging (effective uniform step 2.4·10⁻⁷) holds to N≈9–10.

Everything is committed; the balance-sweep and N≥2 swing-up agents are still running and will refine these into final hardened numbers.

<summary>Agent "Balance precision sweeps N=1-5" completed</summary>
<result>All deliverables are in place. The task is complete.

## Summary

I found the required quantization precision for the BALANCE task across N=1..5, both controllers (FD and Kalman), with LQR tuning, dt scaling, and g scaling. Results are in `results/balance.json` (31 machine-readable records) and `results/balance_notes.md` (human summary with tables and interpretation). Sweep driver: `scripts/sweep_balance.py` (64-core multiprocessing).

### Headline table (best controller, g=9.81, dt=0.01)

| N | best ctrl | max dtheta (rad) | max dv (m/s) |
|---|-----------|------------------|--------------|
| 1 | FD | 0.64 | 0.91 |
| 2 | FD | 0.087 | 0.99 |
| 3 | FD | 0.014 | 0.80 |
| 4 | FD | 0.0025 | 0.69 |
| 5 | FD | 0.00046 | 0.59 |

### Which controller won
**FD (finite-difference) wins** on max_dtheta for N=1-4 and on max_dv everywhere; Kalman only edges ahead on max_dtheta at N=5 (6.6e-4 vs 4.6e-4). Surprising, since the KF "knows" dtheta/dv — but its smoothing adds lag that costs stability margin against pure-quantization (deadband, not Gaussian) error, while the lag-free finite difference does better. Best build = **FD with aggressive LQR (r=0.01, q_theta=100)**, which roughly doubled thresholds vs defaults and was essential for N=4,5.

### Scaling laws
- **vs N:** max_dtheta falls ~6-7x per added link (≈6.3^-N), tracking the growing unstable pole. max_dv decreases only mildly — for N≥3, dv tolerance is ~1000x looser than dtheta. **Angle precision is the binding constraint; velocity-command precision has huge headroom** (joint table: dv non-binding when dtheta ≤ maxdth/10 for N≤3).
- **vs dt:** max_dtheta is essentially **dt-independent** (set by dynamics/basin, not sample rate). max_dv scales **~linearly with dt** (a velocity quantum maps to acceleration error dv/dt per ZOH step). Faster loops don't relax angle precision but tighten velocity precision.
- **vs g:** weak. max_dtheta ~flat (~g^0–0.1); max_dv rises ~g^0.3–0.4. Over a 4x g range thresholds move &lt;2x — second-order vs N.

### Surprises / caveats
- **Limit cycles verified:** N=3 FD at dtheta=0.0125 (just below the 0.0139 threshold) survives but settles into a *stable* quantization limit cycle (base link ~0.11 rad, ~9x the quantum); at 0.02 (above) the cycle is unstable and diverges at t=3.65s. Thresholds are the bounded↔unbounded limit-cycle boundary, not "settle-to-zero." Plots: `runs/traj_N3_limitcycle.png`, `runs/traj_N3_FD.png`.
- **N=5 at dt=0.02 is uncontrollable** with these gains (fails even at the finest grid; recorded as 0/0) — the 15.9/s unstable pole needs dt≤0.01.
- I hit and fixed a real bug: the KalmanBalancer's filter-DARE goes ill-conditioned and raises when dtheta→0 (the bisection's `lo` probe), which crashed the first two runs. Fixed in my sweep via `RobustKalmanBalancer` (floors the KF's internal noise model at 1e-5 while the simulator still applies true quantization) plus a per-task try/except and `lo=1e-6` floor. I did not modify the protected files. Also discovered the default LQR weights (r=0.1, q_theta=10) don't robustly stabilize N=4,5 — tuning to r=0.01, q_theta=100 was necessary.

One note: `balance.json` uses Python-json `Infinity` for non-binding joint dv brackets (loadable by Python; not strict-JSON). Let me know if you need strict-JSON-compliant output.</result>

Self-contained (independent of agent-C files).\n\nUsage: uv run python scripts/trajopt_high.py N T seed [amax] [vmax]\nWrites results/trajectories_high/swingup_N{N}_T{T}_s{seed}.npz on success.\n\"\"\"\n\nimport sys, pathlib\n\nsys.path.insert(0, str(pathlib.Path(__file__).resolve().parents[1]))\nimport numpy as np\nimport casadi as ca\n\nfrom pendulum.dynamics import Chain\n\nG = 9.81\n\n\ndef casadi_thetadd(n, theta, thetad, xdd):\n    \"\"\"Closed-form dynamics in casadi symbols (mirrors Chain.thetadd).\"\"\"\n    i = np.arange(1, n + 1)\n    A = (n - np.maximum.outer(i, i) + 0.5)\n    np.fill_diagonal(A, n - i + 0.25)\n    b = ca.DM(n - i + 0.5)\n    Adm = ca.DM(A)\n    dth = ca.repmat(theta, 1, n) - ca.repmat(theta.T, n, 1)\n    M = Adm * ca.cos(dth) + ca.DM(np.eye(n)) / 12.0\n    rhs = (-xdd) * b * ca.cos(theta) \\\n        - ca.mtimes(Adm * ca.sin(dth), thetad**2) \\\n        + G * b * ca.sin(theta)\n    return ca.solve(M, rhs)\n\n\ndef crosscheck(n, tol=1e-9):\n    ch = Chain(n, G)\n    th = ca.MX.sym(\"th\", n, 1)\n    thd = ca.MX.sym(\"thd\", n, 1)\n    a = ca.MX.sym(\"a\")\n    f = ca.Function(\"f\", [th, thd, a], [casadi_thetadd(n, th, thd, a)])\n    rng = np.random.default_rng(42)\n    for _ in range(20):\n        t0 = rng.uniform(-np.pi, np.pi, n)\n        t1 = rng.uniform(-3, 3, n)

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:55:46; model 15.9s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

The N=6,7 swing-up effort is now running. Status for your animation request:

**What's needed first:** nobody (including agent C, which is still working on N=2–5) has swing-up trajectories for 6–7 links yet — and these are the hardest part. The upright catch basins are tiny (7·10⁻⁴ rad for N=6, 1.4·10⁻⁴ for N=7), so the swing must deliver a 14-dimensional state onto a pinhead.

**What I just launched (18 parallel IPOPT solves):**
1. **Trajectory optimization** — direct collocation (trapezoidal, CasADi/IPOPT) for N ∈ {6,7} × horizon T ∈ {10,12,14} s × 3 randomized initial guesses, with |a| ≤ 40 m/s², |v| ≤ 15 m/s, v(0)=v(T)=0, exact hang→upright boundary conditions. The CasADi dynamics are cross-checked against the verified numpy dynamics before each solve.
2. **TVLQR tracking + catch** (ready and committed) — time-varying LQR along the nominal with terminal-heavy cost, second-order finite-difference rate estimation, hysteretic handoff to the tuned balance LQR that agent A found best (r=0.01, q_θ=100).
3. **Rendering** — the GIF animator is already written; the moment a closed-loop run succeeds I'll render `swingup_N6.gif` and `swingup_N7.gif`.

I'll report when the solves finish (likely minutes to tens of minutes; N=7 may need additional rounds — if the first 9 attempts don't converge I'll try longer horizons, staged homotopy from the N=6 solution, and mesh refinement).

One notable hard limit it found: N=5 is uncontrollable at dt=20 ms no matter the precision (sampling ceiling) — the dt-dependence is real and cliff-like.

### 👤 **User**  ·  17:57:53; waited 25.0s

what's TVLQR?

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:58:01; model 23.9s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

**TVLQR = Time-Varying Linear Quadratic Regulator** — the trajectory-tracking generalization of the LQR we use for balancing.

**Plain LQR** (what holds the pendulum upright): linearize the dynamics about a *fixed point* (upright, $\theta=0$), getting constant matrices $\dot z = Az + Ba$. Minimizing the quadratic cost $\int z^TQz + a^TRa$ yields a constant gain $K$ via the algebraic Riccati equation, and the control law $a = -Kz$ is optimal near that point.

**The problem with swing-up:** there's no fixed point to linearize about — the system sweeps through wildly different configurations (hanging, swinging sideways, inverting). The trajectory optimizer gives us a nominal plan $\big(\theta^{nom}(t), \dot\theta^{nom}(t), a^{ff}(t)\big)$, but you cannot just replay $a^{ff}(t)$ open-loop: the trajectory passes through unstable configurations, so any infinitesimal deviation (integration mismatch, quantization, the collocation grid being coarser than the sim grid) grows exponentially at up to $e^{\lambda t}$ with λ ~ 18–21/s for N=6–7. Open-loop replay diverges mid-swing.

**TVLQR fixes this** by linearizing about *each point of the trajectory* instead of a fixed point:

1. At each time step $k$, linearize the dynamics about $(\theta^{nom}_k, \dot\theta^{nom}_k)$ → time-varying $A_k, B_k$.
2. Run the Riccati equation **backwards in time** from a terminal cost $Q_T$:
$$P_{k} = Q + A_k^T P_{k+1}(A_k - B_kK_k), \qquad K_k = (R + B_k^TP_{k+1}B_k)^{-1}B_k^TP_{k+1}A_k$$
3. Control = feedforward + time-varying feedback on the *deviation* from nominal:
$$a_k = a^{ff}_k + K_k\,(z^{nom}_k - z_k)$$

Intuitively it builds a **funnel around the trajectory**: at every instant, the gain matrix $K_k$ knows the local dynamics (which links are currently unstable, in which directions) and steers deviations back toward the nominal before they can grow. The backward recursion is what makes it anticipatory — the gains at time $t$ already account for how errors would propagate through everything that comes *after* $t$.

**Specifics in our pipeline** (`scripts/closedloop_high.py`): state $z = [\theta, \dot\theta, v]$ (2N+1 dims), input = pivot acceleration; the linearizations come from finite-differencing the verified dynamics along the nominal; and the terminal cost $Q_T$ is set ~1000× heavier than the running cost. That last part is the critical trick for high N: the funnel must *narrow to a point* at the end, because the handoff only works if the state lands inside the upright catch basin — 7·10⁻⁴ rad wide for N=6, 1.4·10⁻⁴ for N=7. A constant-gain LQR couldn't do any of this because no single linearization is valid along the swing.

The full chain is: **collocation** finds the plan → **TVLQR** survives the journey → **balance LQR** holds the destination.

### 👤 **User**  ·  17:59:08; waited 51.0s

oh, so it's like drawing a trajectory through a state space, and adjusting to fit the trajectory?

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  17:59:17; model 21.9s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Exactly right, with two refinements that make the picture precise:

**1. You can't draw just any curve.** The trajectory lives in the full state space ([θ, θ̇, v] — 15-dimensional for N=7) and must be *dynamically feasible*: every point's velocity must be exactly what the physics produces from that state plus some admissible control. That's why finding it is itself an optimization problem (the collocation step) rather than drawing — you're searching the tiny subset of curves connecting "hanging" to "upright" that the physics can actually follow with |a| ≤ 40 m/s² and one measly horizontal actuator. For underactuated systems most curves you'd naively sketch are unreachable: with N links and 1 input, at each instant you control only 1 of the 2N+1 directions of motion — the trajectory must recruit gravity and the chain's own coupling to steer the rest. That's why the solutions look like elaborate pumping motions rather than direct lifts.

**2. "Fit the trajectory" is clock-based, not path-based.** TVLQR doesn't aim at the nearest point of the curve — it tracks a *moving target*: at time t you're supposed to be at z_nom(t), and the feedback pulls you toward where you should be **now**. If you fall behind schedule, it speeds you up rather than letting you trace the same path late. (There are path-based alternatives — time-warping, transverse/orbital stabilization — but plain TVLQR is the schedule-following kind, and at these instability rates being on time matters: the rendezvous with the catch basin is a timed appointment.)

And one more piece of the picture: the correction at each moment isn't "push perpendicular toward the curve." The Riccati matrix P(t) encodes *which* deviations matter — an error along a direction that will explode at e^{20t} gets hammered immediately, an error along a benign direction (like cart velocity mid-swing) is tolerated cheaply. So the funnel around the trajectory isn't a round tube; it's squashed flat in dangerous directions and wide in harmless ones, and its cross-section tightens toward the terminal point where the handoff demands ~10⁻⁴ rad accuracy.

So: optimizer draws the one feasible thread through state space, TVLQR is the moving escort that holds you to the schedule with direction-aware urgency, and the balance LQR takes over at the destination.

Status update on the 6/7-link animations:

**Round 1:** 2 of 9 N=6 solves converged; all N=7 failed. But the N=6 closed-loop test failed — diagnosis: the optimizer found a "whip-crack" solution with link accelerations up to 1840 rad/s². The discrete collocation equations were satisfied to 1e-12, but a trajectory that violent can't be represented accurately on the grid against the true ODE, and even perfect-state TVLQR got thrown out of its stability funnel by t≈2s.

**Round 2 (running now, 11 solves):** added a link-speed bound |θ̇| ≤ 12 rad/s to force *trackable* trajectories, halved the grid to h=0.01, and warm-started: N=6 from its own round-1 solutions, N=7 from the N=6 solution with the 7th link shadowing the 6th (homotopy in N). Fresh random seeds in parallel as backup.

I'll validate in closed loop and render the GIFs as soon as solves land.

<result>All my processes cleaned up. The remaining load (~103) is from other agents on the shared box. The task is complete. Let me give the final report.

## Final Report

**Swing-up achieved and stabilized for all four cases: N = 2, 3, 4, 5.** Each brings the chain from hanging (θ=π) to upright (θ=0 mod 2π) and stabilizes, passing the official `protocol.swingup_trial` with zero quantization across all 4 seeds (horizon = T_swing + 20 s).

### Swing durations &amp; precision thresholds (4 seeds, log-bisection geometric mean)

| N | swing T (s) | max dtheta (rad) | max dv (m/s) | joint dv @ dtheta=thr/10 |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 2 | 3.5  | 7.80e-2 | 1.10e-1 | 1.50e-1 |
| 3 | 5.0  | 8.50e-3 | 3.40e-1 | 2.64e-1 |
| 4 | 7.0  | 9.84e-5 | 1.38e-2 | 7.42e-3 |
| 5 | 12.0 | 6.88e-5 | 2.46e-3 | 3.98e-3 |

All `[lo,hi]` brackets in `results/swingup_traj.json` were re-verified end-to-end: every `lo` succeeds, every `hi` fails, with the final controller.

### What was hard and how it was solved
- **N=4,5 didn't fail to find trajectories — the explicit CasADi dynamics were the bottleneck.** Building thetadd via `ca.solve` (symbolic matrix inverse) blows up combinatorially; a single 120-node N=5 coarse solve took &gt;5 min and didn't converge. Reformulating with **implicit dynamics** (thetadd as a decision variable, `M(theta)·thetadd = rhs` as a constraint) made the same solve take ~5 s and converge from random seeds. This cracked N=5.
- **N=4 needed homotopy warm-starting** (seed N from the N-1 solution by appending a tip link) since random multistarts mostly failed.
- **The decisive controller fix was the state estimator.** A low-pass finite-difference velocity estimate lagged ~half a step and destabilized the high-gain TVLQR required by N≥4 — diverging *even with zero quantization*. Replacing it with a **nonlinear predictor-corrector observer** (predict through the true dynamics using the known applied acceleration, correct on the angle innovation) fixed N=4 and N=5.

### Key findings
- The upright stabilization basin collapses ~5× per added link (measured 0.30 / 0.063 / 0.014 / 0.0033 rad for alternating perturbations), and the max-dtheta swing-up thresholds track it closely. N=5 needs angle precision ~7e-5 rad.
- **dt scaling** (N=2,3): after switching feedforward to time-indexing, N=2 is robust at dt∈{0.005, 0.01, 0.02}; N=3 works at 0.005/0.01 but fails at the coarse 0.02.
- Nothing runs in float32 — the entire pipeline (CasADi/IPOPT, NumPy/scipy, simulator, saved trajectories) is float64; the only low-precision elements are the deliberate `dtheta`/`dv` quantization grids.

### Deliverables written
- `results/trajectories/swingup_N{2,3,4,5}.npz` (t, theta, thetad, a, v, T, target)
- `pendulum/swingup_traj.py` — `SwingupController` + `make_controller_factory` (sim-compatible)
- `pendulum/trajopt.py` — CasADi dynamics (cross-checked to ~1e-13) + explicit &amp; implicit collocation
- `results/swingup_traj.json` — per-N durations, zero-quant success, thresholds, joint points, preset
- `results/swingup_traj_notes.md` — full approach, per-N what-worked/failed, threshold &amp; basin tables, caveats

**Nothing is missing** — all of N=2,3,4,5 are solved.</result>

### 🛠️ **Tool result**  ·  19:12:45; tool 116ms

```
def make_controller_factory(traj_dir, tvlqr_kw=None, balance_kw=None,
                            catch_angle=0.2, catch_rate=2.0):
    """Return make_controller(chain, dt, dtheta, dv) for protocol functions."""
    import pathlib
    traj_dir = pathlib.Path(traj_dir)

    def make(chain, dt, dtheta, dv):
        fn = traj_dir / f"swingup_N{chain.n}.npz"
        return SwingupController(chain, dt, dtheta, dv, str(fn),
                                 tvlqr_kw=tvlqr_kw, balance_kw=balance_kw,
                                 catch_angle=catch_angle, catch_rate=catch_rate)
    return make
```

That confirms the diagnosis precisely. The round-1 N=6 trajectory whips at **θ̇ = 25.7 rad/s** (≈4 rev/s) — the 1840 rad/s² accelerations are centrifugal (Coriolis ∝ θ̇²), not actuator-driven. So my round-2 fix backfired: bounding θ̇ ≤ 12 while needing ≥25 to inject enough energy in 12–14 s made the problem **infeasible** (IPOPT hit "local infeasibility" after 3000 iterations).

The real fix is to give it room to slow the whip down rather than forbid it: longer horizon + higher actuator authority + a heavy smoothness penalty. Let me kill the stragglers and launch a better-tuned N=6-focused round:

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  21:32:57; model 9.9s

To directly answer your question: yes — of the 11 round-2 solves, **5 were N=6** (two warm-started, three fresh seeds) and **6 were N=7**.

That round just finished, and the verdict is clean but negative: **9 of 11 hit IPOPT "local infeasibility,"** including the warm-started ones, after thousands of iterations. The root cause is now pinned down — my round-2 "fix" of capping link speed at θ̇ ≤ 12 rad/s was the mistake: the only swing-up trajectories that exist for 6 links in ~12 s genuinely require whipping at θ̇ ≈ 25 rad/s to pump in enough energy, so the cap made the feasible set empty.

So I've launched **round 3 — 11 solves, N=6 only this time** — with the correct fix: *allow* the fast whip (θ̇ ≤ 18–22) but penalize the acceleration rate so IPOPT prefers the smoothest trackable member of the feasible family, plus longer horizons (14–18 s) and more actuator authority (a_max = 60). Concentrating on N=6 first since it's the more likely win; N=7 follows once I have a working N=6 recipe to homotopy from. I'll validate in closed loop and animate as soon as one converges.

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  21:37:28; model 9.3s

Delivered the whip above — three views:

- **Slow-mo (0.35×)** — best for actually seeing what happens: watch the lower links snap around multiple times while the pivot (which is all we control) darts back and forth, flinging energy up the chain centrifugally.
- **Real-time** — shows just how violent it is; the whole 6-link inversion happens in 10 seconds with the tip moving at a blur.
- **Filmstrip PNG** — 14 stills, good for seeing the configuration sequence frame by frame.

This is the **open-loop nominal** — it satisfies the equations of motion to 1e-12, so it's a perfectly valid swing-up *if executed flawlessly*. The problem is exactly what you can see: at θ̇ ≈ 25.7 rad/s the centrifugal terms (∝ θ̇²) hit ~1840 rad/s², so the dynamics are so stiff there that the tiniest deviation explodes faster than feedback through a single pivot can correct it — which is why closed-loop tracking diverged at t≈2 s. The round-3 batch (still solving) is hunting for a tamer swing that trades this whip for a slower, trackable energy pump.

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  23:35:15; model 16.2s

Here's the full progress picture.

## Done & committed (the core deliverable — complete)

**Dynamics & infrastructure**
- Closed-form N-link dynamics, hand-derived, **verified vs independent sympy Lagrangian** (N=1–5, err <1e-8) and energy conservation (drift <5e-9/10s)
- RK4 simulator with velocity-command actuation and angle/velocity quantization hooks
- Shared precision-evaluation protocol (log-bisection thresholds, multi-seed)

**Task 1 — Balance (N=1–5):** required precision found, both controllers, with dt/g scaling

| N | max δθ (rad) | max δv (m/s) |
|---|------|------|
| 1 | 0.64 | 0.91 |
| 2 | 0.087 | 0.99 |
| 3 | 0.014 | 0.80 |
| 4 | 0.0025 | 0.69 |
| 5 | 4.6·10⁻⁴ | 0.59 |

Key findings: angle precision tightens ~6×/link; velocity precision nearly flat → **sensing is the binding constraint**, not actuation. δθ is ~dt-independent; δv ∝ dt; weak g-dependence. N=5 uncontrollable at dt=20ms (sampling ceiling).

**Task 2 — Swing-up (N=1–5):** all solved (energy-pumping for N=1; collocation + TVLQR + nonlinear observer + catch for N=2–5), independently re-verified, **animations delivered**

| N | swing time | max δθ (rad) | max δv (m/s) |
|---|------|------|------|
| 1 | 0.76s | 0.024 | 0.054 |
| 2 | 3.5s | 0.078 | 0.11 |
| 3 | 5.0s | 0.0085 | 0.34 |
| 4 | 7.0s | 9.8·10⁻⁵ | 0.014 |
| 5 | 12.0s | 6.9·10⁻⁵ | 0.0025 |

Swing-up needs finer precision than balance (catch-basin entry is binding), and the gap widens sharply at N=4–5.

**Theory side:** basin radii + noise-amplification κ computed N=1–7; predicts the ~20×/link angle-precision law; confirmed fp32-θ balances even N=7.

### 🛠️ **Tool result**  ·  23:35:46; tool 100ms

```
def solve_swingup_implicit(
    n, T, K, g=9.81, a_max=40.0, v_max=12.0, theta_target=None,
    settle_frac=0.0, settle_band=0.2, w_a=1.0, w_smooth=1e-3,
    seed=0, init_guess=None, max_iter=3000, print_level=0, tol=1e-7,
):
    """Trapezoidal direct collocation with IMPLICIT dynamics.

    Decision vars per node: theta(n), thetad(n), v, tdd(n), a.
    Constraints: M(theta_k) tdd_k = rhs(theta_k, thetad_k, a_k)   (dynamics)
                 trapezoidal defects on (theta, thetad, v).
    Much faster than the explicit form for n>=4.
    """
    A, b = chain_constants(n)
    if theta_target is None:
        theta_target = np.zeros(n)
    theta_target = np.asarray(theta_target, float)
    h = T / K
    Mfn, rfn = make_M_rhs_fn(n, g)

    opti = ca.Opti()
    TH = opti.variable(n, K + 1)
    TD = opti.variable(n, K + 1)
    VV = opti.variable(1, K + 1)
    TDD = opti.variable(n, K + 1)
    AC = opti.variable(1, K + 1)

    cost = 0
    # node dynamics (implicit) + trapezoidal defects
    for k in range(K + 1):
        opti.subject_to(ca.mtimes(Mfn(TH[:, k]), TDD[:, k])
                        == rfn(TH[:, k], TD[:, k], AC[0, k]))
    for k in range(K):
        opti.subject_to(TH[:, k + 1] - TH[:, k]
                        == 0.5 * h * (TD[:, k] + TD[:, k + 1]))
        opti.subject_to(TD[:, k + 1] - TD[:, k]
                        == 0.5 * h * (TDD[:, k] + TDD[:, k + 1]))
        opti.subject_to(VV[0, k + 1] - VV[0, k]
                        == 0.5 * h * (AC[0, k] + AC[0, k + 1]))
===MAIN===
if __name__ == "__main__":
    for n in range(1, 6):
        err = cross_check(n)
        print(f"N={n}: max thetadd err vs numpy = {err:.2e}")
```

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  23:35:51; model 4.6s

**🔧 tool → Bash**  ·  83ms
```json
{"command": "cd /var/data/inverted-pendulum; sed -n '300,363p' pendulum/trajopt.py", "description": "Read rest of implicit solver: bounds, BCs, return"}
```

### 🛠️ **Tool result**  ·  23:35:51; tool 83ms

```
                        == 0.5 * h * (AC[0, k] + AC[0, k + 1]))
        cost += 0.5 * h * (AC[0, k] ** 2 + AC[0, k + 1] ** 2) * w_a
        cost += w_smooth * (AC[0, k + 1] - AC[0, k]) ** 2

    # boundary
    opti.subject_to(TH[:, 0] == np.pi)
    opti.subject_to(TD[:, 0] == 0)
    opti.subject_to(VV[0, 0] == 0)
    opti.subject_to(TH[:, K] == theta_target)
    opti.subject_to(TD[:, K] == 0)
    opti.subject_to(VV[0, K] == 0)

    opti.subject_to(opti.bounded(-a_max, ca.vec(AC), a_max))
    opti.subject_to(opti.bounded(-v_max, VV, v_max))
    if settle_frac > 0:
        kstart = int(round((1 - settle_frac) * K))
        for k in range(kstart, K + 1):
            opti.subject_to(opti.bounded(theta_target - settle_band,
                                         TH[:, k], theta_target + settle_band))

    opti.minimize(cost)

    rng = np.random.default_rng(seed)
    s = np.linspace(0, 1, K + 1)
    if init_guess is not None:
        Kg = init_guess["theta"].shape[0] - 1
        sg = np.linspace(0, 1, Kg + 1)
        thg = np.array([np.interp(s, sg, init_guess["theta"][:, j]) for j in range(n)])
        tdg = np.array([np.interp(s, sg, init_guess["thetad"][:, j]) for j in range(n)])
        vg = np.interp(s, sg, init_guess["v"])
        ag = np.interp(s, sg, init_guess["a"])
        opti.set_initial(TH, thg)
        opti.set_initial(TD, tdg)
        opti.set_initial(VV, vg.reshape(1, -1))
        opti.set_initial(AC, ag.reshape(1, -1))
    else:
        thg = np.outer(np.full(n, np.pi), (1 - s)) + np.outer(theta_target, s)
        thg += rng.uniform(-0.5, 0.5, thg.shape) * np.sin(np.pi * s)
        opti.set_initial(TH, thg)
        opti.set_initial(TD, rng.uniform(-1, 1, (n, K + 1)))
        opti.set_initial(VV, rng.uniform(-2, 2, (1, K + 1)))
        opti.set_initial(AC, rng.uniform(-5, 5, (1, K + 1)))

    s_opts = {"max_iter": max_iter, "print_level": print_level, "sb": "yes",
              "tol": tol, "acceptable_tol": max(tol * 100, 1e-6),
              "mu_strategy": "adaptive"}
    opti.solver("ipopt", {"expand": True}, s_opts)
    try:
        sol = opti.solve(); status = "solved"
    except RuntimeError:
        sol = opti.debug; status = "failed"

    try:
        THv = np.array(sol.value(TH)); TDv = np.array(sol.value(TD))
        VVv = np.array(sol.value(VV)).ravel(); ACv = np.array(sol.value(AC)).ravel()
        cost_v = float(sol.value(cost))
    except RuntimeError:
        return {"status": "failed", "T": T, "K": K, "n": n}
    return {"t": np.linspace(0, T, K + 1), "theta": THv.T, "thetad": TDv.T,
            "v": VVv, "a": ACv, "cost": cost_v, "status": status,
            "T": T, "K": K, "n": n}


def homotopy_guess(sol_lower):
```

### 🛠️ **Tool result**  ·  23:36:18; tool 90ms

```
def homotopy_guess(sol_lower):
    """Build an initial guess for N links from an (N-1)-link solution by
    appending a copy of the last link's angle/rate (the chain tip), keeping the
    same time grid, control and pivot velocity. The new link starts at pi."""
    th = sol_lower["theta"]; td = sol_lower["thetad"]
    Kp1 = th.shape[0]
    new_th = np.hstack([th, th[:, -1:].copy()])
    new_td = np.hstack([td, td[:, -1:].copy()])
    # ensure new link's start is exactly pi (boundary will enforce anyway)
    new_th[0, -1] = np.pi
    return {"theta": new_th, "thetad": new_td, "a": sol_lower["a"],
            "v": sol_lower["v"]}


if __name__ == "__main__":
```

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  23:40:44; model 33.7s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Two have already converged with excellent numbers (more on that below). To your question — here's how I gauge these, and why one of the three signals above is blank:

## The three levels of "progress"

**1. Is it alive?** `kill -0 $PID` per captured PID (returns success iff the process exists). That's the `4/6 running` line. It tells you *that* it's working, not *how far*.

**2. Did it terminate, and how?** `grep` the log for the markers the script prints — `status=solved`/`status=failed`, `saved`, or IPOPT's `EXIT:` line. Binary, but definitive.

**3. How far through the solve?** IPOPT prints an iteration table (with `print_level=3`), one row per iteration:
```
iter    objective    inf_pr    inf_du    lg(mu)  ...
  45   8.6182e+01  3.21e-04  1.7e+02   -3.1   ...
```
The two columns that matter are **`inf_pr`** (primal infeasibility — how badly the dynamics/collocation constraints are violated) and **`inf_du`** (dual infeasibility — distance from a stationary/optimal point). **Convergence = both driving toward ~1e-8.** If `inf_pr` plateaus at a nonzero value and `mu` stops shrinking → heading for "local infeasibility" (the round-2 failure mode). So you can call a doomed solve early instead of waiting for all 5000 iterations.

## Why level 3 was blank above — and the fix

Python/IPOPT **buffer stdout when writing to a file** (not a TTY), so the iteration table doesn't appear until a flush or process exit — exactly your global rule about `cmd | tail` and pipe buffering, same root cause. To get live rows you force unbuffered output:

```bash
PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1 stdbuf -oL uv run python scripts/gen_n6_agentc.py ... > run.log 2>&1 &
tail -f run.log          # or: Monitor with grep --line-buffered "inf_pr|EXIT"
```

I usually skip live IPOPT rows (they're noisy) and instead watch level 2 plus a **domain metric** computed at the end — for trajectory optimization, "did it converge" is necessary but not sufficient; the real question is *trackability*, which the `max|θ̈|` print answers, and ultimately the closed-loop test. A solve can hit `status=solved` and still be useless (round 3).

## And the actual news

The domain metric just paid off — the two finished agent-C-style solves report **max|θ̈| = 291 rad/s², max|a| = 6.4 m/s², cost = 86** — *gentler than agent C's working N=5* (421 rad/s²). That's squarely in the trackable regime, which is what failed before. Let me run the closed-loop test on them now:

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  23:42:04; model 10.2s

To answer your actual question directly, then the status:

**Gauging script progress** boils down to three levels — *alive* (`kill -0 $PID`), *terminated & how* (grep for `status=`/`saved`/`EXIT:` markers), and *how-far* (IPOPT's live `inf_pr`/`inf_du` columns, which need `PYTHONUNBUFFERED=1 stdbuf -oL` to appear in real time because file output is buffered — same pipe-buffering issue as your `cmd | tail` rule). For optimization specifically, "converged" isn't the goal — I watch a *domain metric* (max|θ̈|, then the closed-loop test), because a solve can report success and still be useless.

That distinction just proved its worth on N=6: the new trajectories converged with great stiffness numbers (291 rad/s², gentler than working N=5) but **still diverged closed-loop**. Isolating it — open-loop replay, then defect comparison — showed the trajectory satisfies its collocation grid to 1e-13 but I'd generated it on a **coarser grid (h=0.0167)** than agent C's proven h=0.01. I'm regenerating at the matched resolution now (6 solves running). That's the kind of bug only the layered diagnosis surfaces — neither "alive" nor "converged" would have caught it.

```
    def __call__(self, theta_meas, t, v, x):
        n = self.n
        theta_meas = np.asarray(theta_meas, float)
        # If the plant/estimate has already diverged (NaN), command 0 so the
        # sim records the failure cleanly instead of raising on int(NaN).
        if not np.all(np.isfinite(theta_meas)) or np.max(np.abs(theta_meas)) > 1e3:
            return v
        theta_est, thetad_est = self._estimate(theta_meas)
        # Bail out cleanly if the run has already diverged (non-finite estimate
        # or the balance KF has gone non-finite). Counts as a failed trial.
        if (not np.all(np.isfinite(theta_est))
                or not np.all(np.isfinite(thetad_est))
                or not np.all(np.isfinite(self.balance.zhat))):
            return v

        # Index the nominal trajectory by wall-clock time so the controller is
        # robust to sim dt != trajectory grid step.
        k = int(round(t / self.dt_nom))
        # Decide phase. Switch to balance when near upright AND past most of traj.
        near = (np.all(np.abs(wrap(theta_est)) < self.catch_angle)
                and np.all(np.abs(thetad_est) < self.catch_rate))
        if self.caught or (near and k >= self.Nsteps - 1):
            if not self.caught:
                self.caught = True
                # seed balance KF state with current estimate (wrapped to upright)
                self.balance.zhat = np.concatenate([wrap(theta_est), thetad_est])
                self.balance.last_a = 0.0
            v_cmd = self.balance(wrap(theta_meas), t, v, x)
            if not np.isfinite(v_cmd):
                return v
            # keep observer's applied acceleration in sync (after dv quantization)
            if self.dv > 0:
                v_cmd = round(v_cmd / self.dv) * self.dv
            self.last_a = (v_cmd - v) / self.dt
            return v_cmd

        # --- TVLQR feedforward+feedback phase ---
        k = min(k, self.Nsteps - 1)
        z_nom = np.concatenate([self.nom_theta[k], self.nom_thetad[k], [self.nom_v[k]]])
        z_est = np.concatenate([theta_est, thetad_est, [v]])
        dz = z_est - z_nom
        a_ff = self.nom_a[k]
        a = a_ff - float((self.Ks[k] @ dz)[0])
        self.k = k
        v_cmd = v + a * self.dt
        if not np.isfinite(v_cmd):
            return v
        if self.dv > 0:
            v_cmd = round(v_cmd / self.dv) * self.dv
        self.last_a = (v_cmd - v) / self.dt
        return v_cmd


def make_controller_factory(traj_dir, tvlqr_kw=None, balance_kw=None,
                            catch_angle=0.2, catch_rate=2.0):
    """Return make_controller(chain, dt, dtheta, dv) for protocol functions."""
    import pathlib
    traj_dir = pathlib.Path(traj_dir)
```

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  00:18:46; model 26.5s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

**This is a genuine structural finding, not a tuning bug.** The controllability ratio collapses to ~1e-13 around t=7.4–8.0 — look at the angles: all six links are within ~20–60°, i.e. the chain is nearly **straight** (just tilted). A straight chain behaves like a single rigid body; its internal bending modes are nearly invisible to and unactuatable from the single pivot. TVLQR responds to near-uncontrollability by demanding enormous gains, which then amplify any error and blow up.

The key insight: the system is *feasible* through that patch (the nominal passes through it fine), you just can't *correct* errors there. So the fix is to stop fighting it — detect low controllability and back off the gains to coast through, rather than letting them explode. Let me see how the controller builds its gains:

**🔧 tool → Bash**  ·  118ms
```json
{"command": "cd /var/data/inverted-pendulum; sed -n '125,167p' pendulum/swingup_traj.py", "description": "Read SwingupController __init__ and TVLQR build"}
```

### 🛠️ **Tool result**  ·  00:18:50; tool 118ms

```
    def __init__(self, chain: Chain, dt, dtheta, dv, traj_file,
                 tvlqr_kw=None, balance_kw=None,
                 catch_angle=0.2, catch_rate=2.0):
        self.chain = chain
        self.n = n = chain.n
        self.dt = dt
        self.dtheta = dtheta
        self.dv = dv
        data = np.load(traj_file)
        self.tnodes = data["t"]
        self.nom_theta = data["theta"]
        self.nom_thetad = data["thetad"]
        self.nom_a = data["a"]
        self.nom_v = data["v"]
        self.target = data["target"] if "target" in data else self.nom_theta[-1]
        self.T = float(self.tnodes[-1])
        self.Nsteps = self.nom_theta.shape[0] - 1
        self.dt_nom = self.T / self.Nsteps   # trajectory grid step

        if tvlqr_kw is None:
            tvlqr_kw = TVLQR_PRESETS[TVLQR_BY_N.get(n, "vtight")]
        elif isinstance(tvlqr_kw, str):
            tvlqr_kw = TVLQR_PRESETS[tvlqr_kw]
        self.Ks = compute_tvlqr(chain, data, dt, **tvlqr_kw)

        bkw = balance_kw if balance_kw is not None else BALANCE_TUNINGS.get(n, {})
        # KalmanBalancer's KF ARE can be ill-conditioned for large dtheta with
        # dv=0 (process noise -> 0). Floor the design noise so the KF is always
        # well posed; the *actual* quantization in the sim is still dtheta/dv.
        dtheta_dsg = min(max(dtheta, 1e-4), 0.05)
        dv_dsg = max(dv, 1e-3)
        self.balance = KalmanBalancer(chain, dt, dtheta_dsg, dv_dsg, **bkw)
        self.balance.dv = dv  # use the real dv for the controller's own quantization

        self.catch_angle = catch_angle
        self.catch_rate = catch_rate
        self.reset()

    def reset(self):
        self.k = 0
        self.caught = False
        self.balance.reset()
        # nonlinear predictor-corrector observer state (continuous angle)
```


```
42:    1: dict(q_theta=100, q_thetad=10, q_x=0.01, q_v=0.1, r=0.01),
43:    2: dict(q_theta=100, q_thetad=10, q_x=0.01, q_v=0.1, r=0.01),
44:    3: dict(q_theta=500, q_thetad=10, q_x=0.001, q_v=0.05, r=0.005),
45:    4: dict(q_theta=10, q_thetad=1, q_x=0.1, q_v=0.5, r=0.1),
46:    5: dict(q_theta=10, q_thetad=1, q_x=0.1, q_v=0.5, r=0.1),
---
def compute_tvlqr(chain: Chain, traj, dt,
                  q_theta=50.0, q_thetad=5.0, q_v=1.0, r=0.5,
                  qf_theta=2000.0, qf_thetad=200.0, qf_v=10.0):
    """Backward Riccati recursion along the nominal trajectory.

    Returns Ks: list of gain matrices (1 x nz) for k=0..N-1 (control nodes).
    States/inputs are deviations from nominal.
    """
    n = chain.n
    nz = 2 * n + 1
    theta = traj["theta"]; thetad = traj["thetad"]; a = traj["a"]
    Nsteps = theta.shape[0] - 1

    Q = np.diag([q_theta] * n + [q_thetad] * n + [q_v])
    R = np.array([[r]])
    Qf = np.diag([qf_theta] * n + [qf_thetad] * n + [qf_v])

    # discretize each segment (Euler is fine at dt=0.01 along smooth traj; use
    # matrix exponential via series for accuracy)
    from scipy.linalg import expm
    Ads = []; Bds = []
    for k in range(Nsteps):
        Ac, Bc = _linearize_z(chain, theta[k], thetad[k], a[k])
        # zero-order-hold discretization
        Mblk = np.zeros((nz + 1, nz + 1))
        Mblk[:nz, :nz] = Ac * dt
        Mblk[:nz, nz:nz + 1] = Bc * dt
        E = expm(Mblk)
        Ad = E[:nz, :nz]
        Bd = E[:nz, nz:nz + 1]
        Ads.append(Ad); Bds.append(Bd)

    P = Qf
    Ks = [None] * Nsteps
    for k in range(Nsteps - 1, -1, -1):
        Ad = Ads[k]; Bd = Bds[k]
        S = R + Bd.T @ P @ Bd
        K = np.linalg.solve(S, Bd.T @ P @ Ad)
        Ks[k] = K
        P = Q + Ad.T @ P @ Ad - Ad.T @ P @ Bd @ K
    return Ks
```

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  00:19:16; model 29.6s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

The gain is the culprit: with fixed `r=0.1`, the Riccati recursion lets gains hit 44,000 at the near-uncontrollable patch. The fix is to **cap the gains** so the controller coasts through that patch instead of exploding. Let me build an N=6 variant that does this, reusing agent C's observer and catch:

**🔧 tool → Write**  ·  49ms
```json
{"file_path": "/var/data/inverted-pendulum/scripts/swingup_n6.py", "content": "\"\"\"N=6 closed-loop swing-up with a controllability-regularized TVLQR.\n\nDiagnosis (see PROGRESS.md): the N=6 swing-up nominal passes through a\nnear-STRAIGHT chain configuration mid-swing (~t/T=0.5) where the system is\nnear-uncontrollable from the single pivot input (ctrb smin/smax ~1e-13). The\nstandard backward-Riccati TVLQR responds with exploding gains (|K|~4e4) that\namplify tiny errors and diverge -- even under perfect state feedback.\n\nFix: cap the feedback-gain magnitude. Through the brief uncontrollable patch\nthe controller cannot correct errors anyway, so it should *coast* (bounded\ngain) and rely on the trajectory's own feasibility; it re-acquires authority\nonce the chain leaves the straight manifold. ### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  00:22:42; model 14.5s
```

While those solve, let me give you the honest state of play, because this has turned into a real research problem.

## What I've established about N=6 swing-up

After methodically eliminating every controller-side explanation, the diagnosis is **rigorous and decisive**:

- The closed-loop diverges at a **fixed point mid-swing (t≈6.8 s), invariant to the controller** — I swept the control penalty `r` over {5, 20, 100, soft} and gain caps over {200…1500}; the divergence time didn't budge by more than 10 ms. When the failure ignores the feedback design entirely, it isn't the feedback.
- **Even perfect state feedback diverges** there, with the TVLQR gains exploding to ~44,000.
- **Why:** the controllability ratio collapses to ~10⁻¹³ at that instant, where all six links are within ~30° of each other — the chain is nearly **straight**. A straight chain moves like a single rigid body, so its internal bending modes become nearly invisible to and unactuatable from the one pivot. No single-input controller can stabilize the transit.

This is consistent with the literature I found earlier: published swing-up tops out at the **triple** pendulum (Glück/Kugi), quadruple is rare, and 5+ is essentially research-frontier. We *did* get N=5 (agent C) — N=6 is genuinely at the edge.

## The current attempt

The only fix is trajectory-level: find a swing-up that **stays curved** (never near-straight) so it remains controllable throughout. The 6 solves now running add an anti-alignment penalty over the mid-swing window to push the chain into S-curved configurations. If one comes back with a healthy controllability ratio, it should track and I'll animate it.

**If this doesn't crack it**, I'd propose one of: (a) keep iterating the curved-trajectory optimization (more seeds/weights, possibly a controllability-constrained formulation), or (b) deliver the rigorous negative result plus the N=6 **balance** animation (holding upright from a small perturbation works fine — that's Task 1, which N=6 passes). I'll report the solve outcome and check with you. Note I've kept strictly to N=6 per your instruction.

## Summary of the N=6 outcome

**Balance (Task 1) — works, animated.** The chain starts leaning 6.9° off vertical (the largest uniform lean it recovers from at g=9.81, dt=0.01) and the pivot-velocity controller drives it back upright and holds it to <0.2°. That's the GIF above.

**Swing-up (Task 2) — rigorous negative result, documented.** Every trackable-stiffness trajectory diverges at a fixed mid-swing instant, and I confirmed by elimination that it's a fundamental obstruction, not a tuning failure:
- divergence time **invariant to the controller** (swept control penalty and gain caps — identical),
- **perfect-state feedback diverges too**, with TVLQR gains exploding to ~44,000,
- because at that instant the chain is **near-straight** and therefore **near-uncontrollable** from a single pivot — the internal bending modes become unactuatable.

This is consistent with the published literature topping out at the triple pendulum; we successfully reached N=5.

### 👤 **User**  ·  01:21:28; waited 2m02s

Let’s try swing up with lower gravity and/or smaller timestep. Do you think it would help?

### 🤖 **Assistant**  ·  01:22:31; model 1m28s

> 💭 (thinking)
> 

Let me reason about it first, because the physics gives a sharp prediction — then test it, because there's enough subtlety that data should settle it.

## The scaling argument (the key point)

There's an **exact symmetry**: the system at gravity $g$ with timestep $dt$ is identical, after rescaling time by $\sqrt{g/L}$, to the system at $g{=}1$ with timestep $dt\sqrt{g/L}$. Angles are dimensionless and invariant. So **lower gravity and smaller timestep are the *same* lever** — both just shrink the one dimensionless parameter $u = dt\sqrt{g/L}$. Asking "lower g and/or smaller dt" is asking the same question twice.

That immediately tells us something important about the N=6 obstruction:

- The failure was a **continuous-time, geometric** property: mid-swing the chain goes near-straight → near-uncontrollable from one pivot → perfect-state TVLQR diverges. Controllability of a configuration is **scale-invariant** — it doesn't care about $g$ or $dt$. So **shrinking $u$ cannot fix the core obstruction.** At lower $g$ the divergence just happens in slower wall-clock time, at the identical dimensionless instant.

## But two caveats make it worth testing

1. **Discretization conditioning.** The perfect-state TVLQR gain exploded to ~44,000 using a `dt=0.01` `expm` discretization of a stiff, near-uncontrollable system. Some of that blowup could be *numerical*, not physical. A genuinely smaller $dt$ would reveal whether the gain is artificially inflated.
2. **Relative actuator authority.** My bounds (`v_max=14`, `a_max=25`) are *fixed numbers*. The natural velocity scale is $\sqrt{gL}$, so at lower $g$ those bounds represent **relatively more** control authority — which could let the optimizer find a tamer, genuinely different (more trackable) trajectory. This part is *not* scale-invariant, because the cost and bounds aren't.

So my honest prediction: **it won't fix the fundamental near-uncontrollable transit** (the decisive perfect-state test should still fail at the same dimensionless config), but it *might* help at the margins via #1 and #2. Let me run the decisive experiment — generate N=6 swing-up at low $g$ and small $dt$, and check whether perfect-state TVLQR still diverges at the same point:

**🔧 tool → Bash**  ·  97ms
```json
{"command": "cd /var/data/inverted-pendulum\ncat > scripts/exp_lowg_n6.py <<'PY'\n\"\"\"Test whether lower gravity / smaller dt helps N=6 swing-up.\nGenerates a nominal at gravity g, then runs PERFECT-STATE TVLQR at timestep dt.\nDecisive question: does it still diverge at the same dimensionless config\n(chain near-straight)? So the N=6 obstruction was **substantially a discretization/sampling limit, not a pure continuous-time uncontrollability** as I'd concluded. My scaling argument was incomplete: the near-uncontrollability is real, but at dt=0.01 it combined with coarse sampling so a single ZOH step over-corrected and threw the state out of the funnel; at dt=0.004 the correction granularity is fine enough to stay in it (even though the gains are *higher*, 69k vs 43k). I was too pessimistic — the wall has a door, and smaller dt opens it.
```

Important caveat before declaring N=6 swing-up solved: this is **perfect-state** feedback. The necessary condition that was *failing* now *passes*, which reopens the problem — but I still need to confirm it survives the realistic observer + quantization. That's very much worth doing now.

The `lowg` case (g=2.4525, dt=0.01) is still solving — and per the exact symmetry, "lower g at fixed dt" ≈ "smaller effective dt," so I'd now **predict lowg also succeeds** (its effective $u = dt\sqrt{g}$ is halved, similar to the smalldt case). That's the clean confirmation. Waiting on it — and then I'll build the realistic closed-loop N=6 swing-up at dt=0.004 and, if it holds, finally animate it.
